## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: chemical kinetics (reaction-rate laws / kinetic ODE modeling)
**Analysis**: The target dA_dt is explicitly the time-derivative of a chemical concentration A, with t as time, which is the standard setup for kinetic rate laws written as ordinary differential equations. With only (t, A) available, the most likely relationships are empirical/mechanistic rate laws where the reaction rate depends on A (and possibly time-varying or externally forced rate constants).

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Zero-order decay: dA_dt = -c0 (constant-rate consumption; A decreases linearly in time)
- First-order decay: dA_dt = -c0 * A (unimolecular decay / pseudo-first-order consumption; exponential A(t))
- Second-order decay: dA_dt = -c0 * A**2 (bimolecular self-reaction; hyperbolic A(t))
- General n-th order rate law: dA_dt = -c0 * A**c1 (empirical reaction order from log-log slope)
- Mixed-order (sum of channels): dA_dt = -c0 * A - c1 * A**2 (parallel first- and second-order pathways)
- Autocatalytic / self-accelerating then limiting: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 - A) (autocatalysis with depletion/limitation by finite capacity)
- Reversible/relaxation-to-equilibrium (linear): dA_dt = -c0 * (A - c1) (approach to equilibrium concentration c1)
- Source + sink (chemostat-like): dA_dt = c0 - c1 * A (constant production/inflow balanced by first-order removal)
- Saturating/inhibited consumption (rational): dA_dt = -c0 * A / (A + c1) (rate saturates at high A due to inhibition/limited sites)
- Substrate inhibition / peaked rate: dA_dt = -c0 * A / (A**2 + c1) (rate decreases again at very high A)
- Time-varying first-order (linear drift): dA_dt = -(c0 + c1 * t) * A (rate constant changes over time, e.g., catalyst deactivation/activation trend)
- Periodic forcing of kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A + c1 * sin(c2 * t) (externally driven inflow/forcing or oscillatory environment)
- Stretched-exponential effective kinetics (state-dependent): dA_dt = -c0 * A * exp(-c1 * t) (effective rate constant decays exponentially in time)
- Nonlinear damping with baseline: dA_dt = -(c0 + c1 * A**c2) * A (concentration-dependent effective rate constant, e.g., crowding/medium effects)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- If dA_dt is proportional to A (straight line when plotting dA_dt vs A), the dynamics are effectively first-order and A(t) is exponential.
- If dA_dt vs A is quadratic-like (curves upward in magnitude with A), it suggests second-order or higher-order kinetics (collision-controlled or bimolecular).
- A nonzero intercept in dA_dt vs A (dA_dt ≠ 0 at A = 0) indicates a source term (inflow/production) or measurement offset; use c0 - c1*A forms.
- A stable fixed point at A = c1 is indicated by dA_dt changing sign around that value; linear relaxation -c*(A-c1) models approach-to-equilibrium.
- If the magnitude of dA_dt increases with A at low A but levels off at high A, use rational saturation forms like -c*A/(A+c1).
- If the magnitude of dA_dt peaks at intermediate A and then decreases at high A, use inhibitory denominators such as -c*A/(A**2+c1).
- If the apparent rate coefficient inferred from -(dA_dt/A) drifts with time t, incorporate an explicit time-varying multiplier (c0 + c1*t) or exp(-c*t).
- Oscillations or sign changes in dA_dt that correlate with t (rather than A) suggest external forcing; include sin(c*t) or cos(c*t) terms in time.
- If A(t) shows rapid early change that slows more than exponential, consider state-dependent effective rate constants (e.g., -(c0 + c1*A^p)*A) capturing medium/crowding effects.

GT Equivalence Verification (CRK26)
============================================================
GT: -c0*A**c1 - c2*A*exp(-c3*_s*t) + c4*_m*sin(sqrt(A))
Candidates evaluated: 79
Matched candidate IDs: []
Matched candidates: []

I need to compare each candidate against the Ground Truth (GT):

**GT:** `-0.33236*A² - 0.33236*A*exp(-0.07071*t) + 0.13650*sin(sqrt(A))`

The GT has three terms:
1. A polynomial term: `-c₀*A²`
2. An exponential decay term: `-c₂*A*exp(-c₃*_s*t)`
3. A sinusoidal term: `c₄*_m*sin(sqrt(A))`

The key challenge is the `sin(sqrt(A))` term. This is a transcendental function that cannot be exactly represented by polynomials, logarithms, rational functions, or simple exponentials of A. Let me check which candidates contain `sin(sqrt(A))` or something equivalent.

Looking through all 79 candidates, I need to find ones that contain `sin(sqrt(A))` or can produce it.

**Key observation:** `sin(sqrt(A))` is a specific transcendental function. It cannot be exactly represented by:
- Polynomials in A (any power)
- `log(A*c + 1)` terms
- `exp(A*c)` terms
- Rational functions of A
- `sin(A*c + c)` (this is sin of A, not sin of sqrt(A))
- Powers like `A^c` for any fixed c

Let me scan for candidates with `sin(sqrt(A))`:
- None of the candidates contain `sin(sqrt(A))`.

**Candidates with `sin`:** Candidates 23, 24, 68 have `sin(A*c + c)`, which is `sin` of a linear function of A, NOT `sin(sqrt(A))`. These are fundamentally different functions and cannot match.

**Candidates with `A^c` (free exponent):** Candidates 1, 19, 22, 46, 50, 75, 77, 78, 79, 28 have terms like `A^c5*c6`. Could `A^c` approximate `sin(sqrt(A))`? No - `A^c` is a power function (monotonic for A>0), while `sin(sqrt(A))` is oscillatory. They are fundamentally different and cannot be exactly equal for all A≥0.

**Candidates with `log`:** Many candidates use `log(A*c + 1)`. This is monotonic and cannot equal `sin(sqrt(A))`.

**Candidates with `exp(A*c)`:** Some candidates have exponential terms in A. These grow/decay monotonically and cannot match `sin(sqrt(A))`.

**Rational function candidates (many):** These produce rational functions of A combined with `exp(c*t)`. Rational functions cannot produce `sin(sqrt(A))`.

Since `sin(sqrt(A))` is an essential, non-eliminable term in the GT (its coefficient is ~0.1365, not zero), and NO candidate contains `sin(sqrt(A))` or any function that could be algebraically equivalent to it, none of the candidates can match the GT.

Let me verify there's no hidden way to get `sin(sqrt(A))`: The Taylor series of `sin(sqrt(A))` = `sqrt(A) - A^(3/2)/6 + A^(5/2)/120 - ...` is an infinite series in half-integer powers. A finite sum of such powers cannot equal the infinite series exactly.

For candidates with terms like `A^(3/2)`, `sqrt(A)`, `A^(5/2)` - these provide only finitely many terms of the Taylor expansion, so they cannot exactly match `sin(sqrt(A))`.

Going through each candidate systematically, every single one lacks the ability to produce `sin(sqrt(A))` exactly.
